Revenues – Current Expenditures = Net Operating Balance (NOB)

NOB + Capital Expenditures = Overall Balance (OB)

OB = External Financing + Domestic Financing

OB = Ext. Fin.+ Dom. Bank Fin. + Dom. Non-Bank Fin.

OB = Ext. Fin. + CB Fin. + Com. Banks Fin. + Dom. Non-Bank Fin.

# Debt Dynamics

# Overall balance and debt:

Total revenues and grants

#

Total expenditures

二

Overall balance

Financing:

$$
D _ {t} - D _ {t - 1} = - O B _ {t}
$$

if  $OB > 0$  assets  $\uparrow$  or debt  $\downarrow$ : net debt

if  $OB <   0$  assets  $\downarrow$  or debt  $\uparrow$  net debt

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} - O B _ {t}
$$

# Debt Dynamics

# Primary balance and debt:

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} - O B _ {t}
$$

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} - \left(R _ {t} - N I E _ {t} - I _ {t}\right)
$$

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} + I _ {t} - (R _ {t} - N I E _ {t})
$$

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} + i D _ {t - 1} - P B _ {t}
$$

$$
D _ {t} = (1 + i) D _ {t - 1} - P B _ {t}
$$

Autonomous component of debt dynamic

Primary balance, PB

Nominal interest rate

# Debt Dynamics

# Debt dynamics in terms of GDP ratios: Overall Balance

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} - O B _ {t}
$$

$$
\frac {D _ {t}}{G D P _ {t}} = \frac {D _ {t - 1}}{G D P _ {t}} - \frac {O B _ {t}}{G D P _ {t}}
$$

$$
\frac {D _ {t}}{G D P _ {t}} = \left(\frac {G D P _ {t - 1}}{G D P _ {t}}\right) \frac {D _ {t - 1}}{G D P _ {t - 1}} - \frac {O B _ {t}}{G D P _ {t}}
$$

$$
\frac {G D P _ {t}}{G D P _ {t - 1}} = 1 + n
$$

$$
\frac {D _ {t}}{G D P _ {t}} = \frac {1}{1 + n} \frac {D _ {t - 1}}{G D P _ {t - 1}} - \frac {O B _ {t}}{G D P _ {t}}
$$

$$
d _ {t} = \frac {1}{1 + n} d _ {t - 1} - o b _ {t}
$$

Evolution of debt ratio depends on:

Overall balance ratio  
(Nominal) GDP growth

# Debt Dynamics

# Primary balance and debt:

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} - O B _ {t}
$$

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} - \left(R _ {t} - N I E _ {t} - I _ {t}\right)
$$

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} + I _ {t} - (R _ {t} - N I E _ {t})
$$

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t - 1} + i D _ {t - 1} - P B _ {t}
$$

$$
D _ {t} = (1 + i) D _ {t - 1} - P B _ {t}
$$

Autonomous component of debt dynamic

Primary balance, PB

Nominal interest rate

# Debt Dynamics

Debt dynamics in terms of GDP ratios: Primary Balance

$$
d _ {t} = \left(\frac {1 + i}{1 + n}\right) d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t}
$$

$$
1 + i = (1 + r) (1 + \pi)
$$

$$
1 + n = (1 + g) (1 + \pi)
$$

$r$  is real interest rate  $g$  is real growth rate  $\pi$  is GDP deflator inflation

$$
d _ {t} = \left(\frac {1 + r}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1}\right) - p b _ {t}
$$

Autonomous component of debt dynamic

# Debt Dynamics

# Debt dynamics in terms of GDP ratios: Autonomous component

Consider the case where  $pb = 0$  (i.e., we abstract from fiscal policy):

Year t  
![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/56700fe27e18d432f2b545ea7610be71e61d2a13deac306e3308fbf85e681fb4.jpg)  
GDP growth is the rate at which GDP grows

# Debt Dynamics

# Debt dynamics in terms of GDP ratios: Autonomous component

Think of debt-to-GDP as an aerostatic balloon:

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/883b459be2c151a10b7794deb6f4db4c85bf2d7aea5784e8c9badf670397be48.jpg)

Interest rate is like hot air: it pushes debt-to-GDP up

GDP growth is like the sand bags: it helps bring the debt-to-GDP down

If interest rate  $>$  GDP growth, debt-to-GDP tend to  $\uparrow$  If interest rate  $<$  GDP growth, debt-to-GDP tend to

# Debt Dynamics—Useful Formulas

# Decomposition of debt dynamics:

From  $d_{t} = \frac{1 + r}{1 + g} d_{t - 1} - p b_{t}$  the change in debt becomes

$$
d _ {t} - d _ {t - 1} = \frac {r - g}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t}
$$

One can attribute the change in debt-to-GDP to:

- Interest rates  $\frac{r}{1 + g} d_{t - 1}$  
Growth  $-\frac{g}{1 + g} d_{t - 1}$  
Fiscal policy  $- p b_{t}$

# Debt Dynamics—Useful Formulas

# The debt-stabilizing primary balance:

If government wants to keep debt stable so that  $d_{t} = d_{t - 1}$  then:

$$
d _ {t - 1} = \frac {1 + r}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t} \quad \Longrightarrow \quad p b _ {t} = \frac {1 + r}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1} - d _ {t - 1}
$$

$$
p b _ {t} = \frac {r - g}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1}
$$

- The larger the difference between  $r$  and  $g$  and the larger the initial debt, the greater the primary surplus/deficit needed to stabilize the debt  
- One can revert the formula to compute at which level debt will stabilize if a certain balance was kept forever

# Debt Dynamics—Useful Formulas

# Deriving the primary balance for targeting a debt level:

Consider the formula of the debt dynamic:

$$
d _ {t} = \left(\frac {1 + r}{1 + g}\right) d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t} \quad d _ {t} = \beta d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t}
$$

Starting from  $d_0$ :  $d_1 = \beta d_0 - p b_1$ $d_2 = \beta^2 d_0 - \beta p b_1 - p b_2$

$$
d _ {3} = \beta^ {3} d _ {0} - \beta p b _ {1} - \beta p b _ {2} - p b _ {3} \quad \dots \quad d _ {t} = \beta^ {t} d _ {0} - \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {t} \beta^ {t - i} p b _ {i}
$$

Given  $d_0$ : What will debt be if the  $pb$  is kept constant forever?

Given  $d_0$ : What is the constant  $pb$  that allows reaching a target debt to GDP  $d^*$  in  $T$  years?

# Debt Dynamics—Useful Formulas

# Deriving the primary balance for targeting a debt level:

From  $d_{t} = \beta^{t}d_{0} - \sum_{i = 1}^{t}\beta^{t - i}pb_{i}$  it follows that

$$
d _ {t} = \beta^ {t} d _ {0} - p b \frac {1 - \beta^ {t}}{1 - \beta}
$$

If  $\beta < 1$  then debt will eventually converge to

$$
d ^ {*} = - p b \frac {1}{1 - \beta}
$$

The constant  $pb$  that allows reaching a target debt to GDP  $d^{*}$

in  $T$  years is

$$
p b = \frac {1 - \beta}{1 - \beta^ {t}} \left(\beta^ {t} d _ {0} - d ^ {*}\right)
$$

# Debt Dynamics—Useful Formulas

# Adding foreign currency/external debt:

Suppose part of debt is denominated in foreign currency. Let:

- $e_t$  be the exchange rate at time  $t$  (units of local currency per 1 unit of foreign currency)  
- $i^F$  be the interest rate on foreign currency denominated debt, including risk premium  
- $D_{t}$  be total debt,  $D_{t}^{N}$  be debt denominated in local currency, and  $D_{t}^{F}$  be the local currency value of the debt denominated in foreign currency

With this notation, the debt stock comprising domestic and foreign-currency debt can be written as:

$$
D _ {t} = D _ {t} ^ {N} + e _ {t} D _ {t} ^ {F}
$$

$D^{N} =$  Local currency-denominated debt

$DF =$  Foreign currency-denominated debt

$e =$  Nominal exchange rate (local currency per  $\S$ )

# Debt Dynamics—Useful Formulas

# Adding foreign currency/external debt (continued):

Let  $\varepsilon_{t}$  be the exchange rate depreciation and  $\alpha_{t}$  be the share of debt denominated in foreign currency to total debt

$$
D _ {t} = (1 + i) \left(1 - \alpha_ {t - 1}\right) D _ {t - 1} + \left(1 + i ^ {F}\right) \left(1 + \varepsilon_ {t}\right) \alpha_ {t - 1} D _ {t - 1} - P B _ {t}
$$

Expressing all nominal variables in terms of GDP this becomes:

$$
d _ {t} = \left[ (1 + i) \left(1 - \alpha_ {t - 1}\right) + \left(1 + i ^ {F}\right) \left(1 + \varepsilon_ {t}\right) \alpha_ {t - 1} \right] \frac {d _ {t - 1}}{1 + n} - p b _ {t}
$$

$$
1 + \left(i \left(1 - \alpha_ {t - 1}\right) + i ^ {F} \alpha_ {t - 1}\right) + \left(1 + i ^ {F}\right) \varepsilon_ {t} \alpha_ {t}
$$

$$
d _ {t} = \frac {1 + i ^ {*}}{1 + n} d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t} + \frac {\left(1 + i ^ {F}\right)}{1 + n} \varepsilon_ {t} \alpha_ {t - 1} d _ {t - 1}
$$

Average interest rate  $(i^{*})$

Equation similar to previous, except for effect of  $\varepsilon$  on interest payment and stock of debt

# Debt Dynamics

# Adding foreign currency/external debt (continued):

Remembering that we could rewrite

$$
d _ {t} = \left(\frac {1 + i}{1 + n}\right) d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t} \quad \text {a s}
$$

$$
d _ {t} = \left(\frac {1 + r}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1}\right) - p b _ {t}
$$

We can express the corresponding formula with foreign currency debt as

$$
d _ {t} = \left(\frac {1 + r ^ {*}}{1 + g} d _ {t - 1} - p b _ {t} + \frac {\left(1 + i ^ {F}\right)}{1 + n} \varepsilon_ {t} \alpha_ {t - 1} d _ {t - 1} \right.
$$

# Debt Analysis

# Debt Sustainability Analysis

# Basic approach:

Project debt in the medium and long term under:

- Current fiscal policy, including announced changes that will credibly be implemented  
- Projections on long-term interest rates, growth rates, exchange rate, composition of debt, etc.

Consider also the major fiscal and macroeconomic vulnerabilities and project debt under different shocks scenarios:

- Increase interest rates  
Decrease growth  
Depreciation  
- Materialize contingent liabilities  
- Worse fiscal balances

Is debt sustainable under current policies? Does it explode? Will it stay below or breach comfortable levels under shocks?

# Debt Sustainability Analysis

# Debt thresholds in IMF/WB DSA for LICs:

Countries operating in a weaker institutional and policy environment are likely to experience debt distress at significantly lower debt ratios.

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Debt-burden indicators for external public debt</td><td colspan="3">Assessment of institutional strength and quality of policies</td></tr><tr><td>Weak</td><td>Medium</td><td>Strong</td></tr><tr><td>1. PV of Debt/GDP</td><td>30</td><td>40</td><td>50</td></tr><tr><td>2. PV of Debt/Exports</td><td>100</td><td>150</td><td>200</td></tr><tr><td>3. PV of Debt/Revenue</td><td>200</td><td>250</td><td>300</td></tr><tr><td>4. Debt service/Exports</td><td>15</td><td>20</td><td>25</td></tr><tr><td>5. Debt service/Revenue</td><td>25</td><td>30</td><td>35</td></tr></table>

# Debt Sustainability Analysis

# What are Present Values (PVs)?

- Debt stock can be thought of as the sum of all future principal (amortization) payments  
- Present value is the sum of all future discounted debt service payments  
- Why are PVs used? Because they capture the concessionality of debt:

A loan that is repaid in 40 years, at a low interest rate, is much less burdensome than a loan that has to be repaid next year at a high interest rate.  

- The nominal debt stock computed today is indifferent between these two loans if the outstanding debt is the same, but the PV captures that the first loan is less burdensome.

# ORIGINAL PATH

# An Introduction to Financial Programming

Carlos G Fernández Valdovinos

Spring 2023

# Introduction

Financial Programming (FP) is a framework used to design a set of macroeconomic policies aimed at achieving or maintaining macroeconomic stability objectives. (IMF, 2015)

# Three steps for (FP)

- Diagnose the economy by looking macroeconomic accounts  
Projection of economic variables  
Design of policies to achieve objectives

Financial programming is a tool for ensuring accounting and economic consistency in economic analysis and projections.

Accounting consistency: One variable per sector as residual. Also cross-checks across accounts.

Economic consistency: What is the "story"? Does it make sense? What policies are assumed?

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/3338f0f09e1b6e1970633afc83454e96801040c1ace3b09ec94d90be2e908011.jpg)

# An Introduction to Financial Programming

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/21475d0aa9b0efae48457a7d6dbde1b6791914aa0cb67aff48bb296b5112d76c.jpg)

# A caveat > INTERSECTORAL DISCREPANCIES

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/826dfce5f9fc9ad000d5b522046da19e043e54ebb45f9ae7e88e9c6a5fdc8a1c.jpg)

# GIVEN INCONSISTENCIES, CHOICES MUST BE MADE.

# What is Financial Programming?

In a nutshell, it is what the IMF does……

To forecast the macroeconomy  
$\succ$  With the objective of helping to preserve macroeconomic stability

# External Sector

External sector refers to transactions with "the rest of the world" or non-residents of a country.

The interaction between an economy and the rest of the world includes: (i) goods and services transactions; and (ii) financial transactions.

# Balance of Payment (BOP) Records

# Real transactions

Flows of goods, services, and income, including transfers.

-Current Account

a. Account on goods  
b. Account on services  
c. Factor income account  
d. Current transfers account

# Financial transactions

Transactions in assets, and the creation, repayment or extinction of claims and liabilities.

Financial or Capital Account

a. Direct investments  
b. Portfolio investments  
c. Other investment  
d. Reserve assets

# Balance of Payment Accounting Methodology

INTERNATIONAL MONETARY FUND

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/011261eb77a352325fdca63d81857f967ab58c95e2952b409287e59ea7879e96.jpg)

Balance of

Payments and

International

Investment

Position Manual

Sixth Edition (BPM6)

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/5bc0cb3f1d895e36fd1d7ea1037f3ba6d42259fd7c28aeb2e232d0a64bad42fb.jpg)

# Double-Entry Accounting

The BOP records both sides of a transaction: (i) the value of what is transacted; (ii) how it is paid.

# Credits (+)

- Exports of goods & services  
- Return accrued for providing labor, financial assets, and natural resources to nonresidents.  
- Disposal of assets (gold and claims) on the world  
- Incurrence of liabilities to the world  
Donations received

# Debits (-)

- Imports of goods & services  
- Return payable for using labor, financial assets, and natural resources for non-residents.  
Acquisition of assets (gold and claims) on the world  
Decrease in liabilities to the world  
Donations made

However, in practice the sum of credits is not equal to the sum of debits, thus there is an account called Errors and Omissions to compensate for the differences.

Balance of Payment = Current Account + Capital Account

$$
B O P = C A B + K A B
$$

# Current Account = Trade in Goods + Trade in Services + Net Current Transfers + Net Factor Income

$$
T r a d e \quad i n \quad G o o d s = (X _ {g o o d s} - M _ {g o o d s})
$$

$$
\text {T r a d e i n S e r v i c e s} = \left(X _ {\text {s e r v i c e s}} - M _ {\text {s e r v i c e s}}\right)
$$

$$
\text {C u r r e n t A c c o u n t} = X - M + N C T + N F I
$$

$$
C A B = X - M + N C T + N F I
$$

# Kapital Account Balance = Net Official Foreign Financing + Net Private Capital Flows + Net Short Term Capital Flows

$$
K A B = N O F F + N P K F + N S T K F
$$

Finally,

$$
\text {B a l a n c e o f P a y m e n t} = \text {C A B} + \text {K A B}
$$

$$
B O P = C A B + K A B
$$

$$
B O P = C A B + E x t. F i n. G o v. + E x t. F i n. P r v. S e c t o r
$$

$$
B O P = - \Delta N e t F o r g i n A s s e t s
$$

(i) Current Account deficits means a country spends more (less) on goods of other countries that it receives.  
(ii) A current account deficit needs to be financed, therefore implying a capital inflow.  
(iii) If normal flows don't cover the deficit in CA, then the finance is done by using reserves or via official financing, for instance from the IMF.

Basic structure of the Balance of Payment:

Current Account

Capital and Financial Account

Overall Balance

Financing

# Real Sector

# Real sector refers to real economic transactions of a country.

# National Accounts Methodology

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/346bd0d41c0ad07093839943a967750c9f14148f5117cc1ec886a55a1e9da41f.jpg)

Gross Domestic Product (GDP): the market value of all final goods and services produced within a country in a given period.

Gross National Income (GNI) measures production or income that is earned using a country's resources.

Gross National Disposable Income (GNDI) is the total income available to residents for either final consumption or saving.

$$
G D P = C + I + (X - M)
$$

GNI = GDP + Factor Income from Nonresidents – Factor Income to Nonresidents

GNI = GDP + Net Factor Income from Nonresidents

GNDI = GNI + Current Transfers from Nonresidents – Current Transfers to Nonresidents

GNDI = GNI + Net Current Transfers from Nonresidents

(In trillions of liras, at current prices)

# Several approaches to measure Gross Domestic Product

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/e0df189e84dab489e8e4fd9d1d64da031ee4317ed8b2d43a7d70a3a0f8df783c.jpg)

$$
G D P = C + I + (X - M)
$$

$$
G D P - C - I = (X - M)
$$

$$
G D P + N e t F a c t o r I n c o m e - C - I = (X - M) + N e t F a c t o r I n c o m e
$$

$$
G N I - C - I = (X - M) + \text {N e t F a c t o r I n c o m e}
$$

$$
G N I + N e t C u r r e n t T r a n s. - C - I = (X - M) + N F I + N e t C u r r e n t T r a n s.
$$

$$
G N D I - C - I = (X - M) + N F I + N C T
$$

$$
(G N D I - C) - I = (X - M) + N F I + N C T
$$

$$
S - I = (X - M) + N F I + N C T
$$

$$
S - I = \text {C u r r e n t A c c o u n t B a l a n c e}
$$

$$
S - I = \text {C u r r e n t A c c o u n t B a l a n c e}
$$

$$
(S p - I p) + (S g - I g) = \text {C u r r e n t A c c o u n t B a l a n c e}
$$

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/d7710f684ca396ae5a09f01d5959b84ee3b114e0e2fd7b309d6dd3ba13001fa2.jpg)

When a country saves "too little" ( $S < 1$ ), it runs a current account deficit

Famous "TWIN DEFICITS"

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/d38a19b46903de7d849a7e321c15cea23e071a9b6eded7f8da8caba25bb2ed3a.jpg)  
U.S. example: Saving, Investment and Current Account Balance

# Government Sector

# Government sector refers to real and financial transactions of the government in a given country.

# The Government Sector Activities

- Collect taxes (revenues)  
- Produce goods and services (non-market)  
- Redistribute wealth  
Financing (to/from other sectors of the economy)

# Four key accounting principles:

- Coverage: General Government & Public Corporations  
- Accrual Basis: flows are recorded when the transaction is made, not necessarily when payments are made  
- Consolidation: transactions between units of the same sector should be netted out to reflect transactions with other sectors  
- Valuation: at market value

# Government Accounting Methodology

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/745dcd5c26dac5064ca2f94c31b13188ebd95b415db1e1af7bf7a4b47b0b392e.jpg)

# Revenues - Current Expenditures = Net Operating Balance (NOB)  

NOB + Acquisition of Non-financial Assets = Net Lending/Borrowing  
Net Lending/Borrowing = Net Acquisition of Financial Assets +  
Net Incurrence of Liabilities

Revenues – Current Expenditures = Net Operating Balance (NOB)

NOB + Capital Expenditures = Overall Balance (OB)

OB = External Financing + Domestic Financing

OB = Ext. Fin.+ Dom. Bank Fin. + Dom. Non-Bank Fin.

OB = Ext. Fin. + CB Fin. + Com. Banks Fin. + Dom. Non-Bank Fin.

# Monetary Sector

# Monetary sector refers to financial transactions of the financial sector in a given country.

# Financial Sector Functions

Intermediates and facilitates resources flowing across economic sectors.  

- Serves as a bridge between sectors with surplus of financial resources (savers) to those with deficit of financial resources (borrowers).  
- Creates saving vehicles that allow for the intertemporal allocation of consumption.  
- Creates credit instruments that allow for the allocation of savings to investment.  
- Creates products that mitigate financial risk.

# DEPOSITORY CORPORATIONS

- Central Bank  
- Commercial Banks, Credit Unions, Savings and Loans, etc  
Money Market Funds

# OTHER FINANCIAL CORPORATIONS

Insurance companies  
Pension funds  
Mutual funds  
Leasing companies and others

# MONETARY STATISTICS

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/cc63287b01cc6ddf6622c96d249891251a0f857b2f93e3f98c152bf41856ba97.jpg)

# FUNCTIONS

Serves as lender of last resort  

- Issues currency  
- Conducts monetary policy  
- Holds the country's foreign reserves  
- Acts as banker to government  
Oversees the financial sector

# STOCKS

$$
B M = N F A + N D A
$$

$$
B M = N F A + D C G + D C P S + O I N
$$

# FLOWS

$$
\Delta B M = \Delta N F A + \Delta N D A
$$

$$
\Delta B M = \Delta N F A + \Delta D C G + \Delta D C P S + O I N
$$

# Flow of Funds

The links between the four different sectors could be systematically described in a flow of funds account.

The flow of funds account provides a comprehensive framework for describing the interrelations among macroeconomic accounts.

# KEYIDEAS

Sectors engage in non-financial transactions & financial transactions.

For each sector, the sum of all transactions (financial and non-financial) is zero.

For a given transaction, the sum across all sectors is zero.

![](https://cdn-mineru.openxlab.org.cn/result/2025-12-02/e320e3ce-bce3-4ea4-b81b-eb889a99b911/b310185251882af88899c4877d86b3148abc89f3aa21be43819effe2a14d7ce9.jpg)

The flow of funds table is a matrix: columns represent the four different sectors while rows represent the different type of transactions.

Each column add-up to zero: sum of non-financial and financial transactions for a sector is zero.

Each row add-up to zero: increased liabilities (more spending) for a given sector is equal to increased assets (less spending) of other sector.

$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta B M = \Delta N F A + \Delta N D A \\ \Delta B M = \Delta N F A + \Delta D C G + \Delta D C P S + \Delta O I N \\ \end{array}
$$

# Main Identities

NOB = Revenues - Current Exoenditures

Overall Balance = NOB + Capital Expenditures

Overall Balance = (Net) Foreign Financing + (Net) Domestic Financing
